<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_18676271">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18676271" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/901806-morgan-mcguire" class="left imgcol" title="Morgan McGuire"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1450104900p2/901806.jpg" alt="Morgan McGuire" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/18676271?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 26, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/901806-morgan-mcguire" class="user" title="Morgan McGuire">Morgan McGuire</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/965402.Roofworld" title="Paperback 9780099623403" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer18676271">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12297271159276876953">I wanted to like this book because it has a clever plot, but the author didn't bring the various plot threads and characters together soon enough or make them compelling, so I eventually got sick of seeing vignettes instead of a story.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_151978402">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/151978402" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4164688-paul" class="left imgcol" title="Paul"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1410469492p2/4164688.jpg" alt="Paul" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/151978402?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 11, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4164688-paul" class="user" title="Paul">Paul</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/965402.Roofworld" title="Paperback 9780099623403" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer151978402">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6342532024253454929">The premise Roofworld by Christopher Fowler is a relatively simple one. For many decades a secret society has existed on the rooftops of London. They live like ghosts and the majority of people are oblivious to their presence.<br /><br />Enter Robert Linden, an executive for a production company, tasked by his boss to locate novels that can be adapted for the screen. During one such search, Robert meets a woman called Rose Leonard and they are accidentally made aware of the Roofworld’s existence. Circumstan</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6342532024253454929">The premise Roofworld by Christopher Fowler is a relatively simple one. For many decades a secret society has existed on the rooftops of London. They live like ghosts and the majority of people are oblivious to their presence.<br /><br />Enter Robert Linden, an executive for a production company, tasked by his boss to locate novels that can be adapted for the screen. During one such search, Robert meets a woman called Rose Leonard and they are accidentally made aware of the Roofworld’s existence. Circumstances lead them further and further into this alien sub-culture and they are caught in the midst of a power struggle between two opposing forces. On one side, a group of displaced individuals trying to make a life for themselves away from politics and bureaucracy of the world below. On the other, a Satanic cult lead by a mad man who wants nothing but power.<br /><br />Meanwhile at ground level, the police are struggling to understand the nature of the war that has erupted on London’s rooftops. As bodies literally start dropping from the sky, Detective Inspector Ian Hargreave is called upon to try and make sense of the ongoing slaughter.<br /><br />The two main characters in Roofworld are well realised. Robert and Rose are polar opposites of one another. He is reserved and somewhat staid, while she is outgoing and curious. You just know that if it wasn’t for this adventure they find themselves on, they would have little in common and never mix in the same social circles. Robert has never taken a chance in his life and is shaken from his cosy existence when Rose comes into his life. Their relationship is realistically illustrated by Fowler.<br /><br />The man trying to maintain order in the Roofworld society is a disgraced Dr called Nathaniel Zalian. He is flawed and troubled by his own inner demons but tries to do the best for all those that find their way to the group.<br /><br />The leader of the cult is Chymes. This character is the only disappointment in the novel as far as I am concerned. He is suitably violent, mysterious, and evil, but I have always felt like there was more to this man that was never fully explored. It would have been nice to get more details about his motivations and the beginnings of his rise to power. This slight criticism aside, his maniacal qualities do make him a marvellous nemesis.<br /><br />The thing I still remember from when I first read the novel was that I was utterly engrossed by the society that the author had created. Even now, years later I continue to be impressed by the lengths Fowler has gone to in order to create something that is believable. From the descriptions of the gadgets used to travel across the rooftops, to the explanations of how the society has managed to exist. As London has altered and changed over the years, the members of the community have needed to adapt with it. There is even detail about what happens to those that get told old to live on the roofs anymore.<br /><br />The novels climax is truly spectacular and the use of a famous London landmark is the icing on the cake. I have always maintained, and still do, that this would make a great piece of cinema. It has everything that you could ever want from a thriller. The novel only covers a period of nine days but in that time, the action is non-stop.<br /><br />It’s scary to think that I last read the novel over twenty years ago. The good news is that the story has aged well, and with the exception of couple of minor references to out of date computer technology, everything still feels as fresh is did two decades ago. Fowler’s social commentary on the homeless and dispossessed that existed in London in the Eighties is as relevant today as it was then. If you have never read Roofworld I suggest you check it out.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6342532024253454929">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1170355005">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1170355005" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/36412681-soho-black" class="left imgcol" title="Soho_black"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" alt="Soho_black" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1170355005?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 16, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/36412681-soho-black" class="user" title="Soho_black">Soho_black</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/965402.Roofworld" title="Paperback 9780099623403" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1170355005">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7688837635814935422">Have you noticed how little of our lives is spent looking upwards? When you’re in a city, whether for pleasure, or on your way to and from work, how often do you look up? By “look up”, I don’t just mean a glance up to check the weather, but really take a look at the top of the building?<br /><br />And if you did happen to look up there, and see someone sliding from roof to roof hanging from only a wire between rooftops, what would you think? Would you think that it looked like fun and want to join them? Wou</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7688837635814935422">Have you noticed how little of our lives is spent looking upwards? When you’re in a city, whether for pleasure, or on your way to and from work, how often do you look up? By “look up”, I don’t just mean a glance up to check the weather, but really take a look at the top of the building?<br /><br />And if you did happen to look up there, and see someone sliding from roof to roof hanging from only a wire between rooftops, what would you think? Would you think that it looked like fun and want to join them? Would you think they were crazy and in danger of falling? Would it merely provide a fleetingly attractive alternative to the slow moving rush hour traffic or the crush of people on the Underground?<br /><br />Robert Linden is amongst the first group. His job is to find authors and gain the film rights to their books. Having found a novel he thinks might be suitable for this purpose, he seeks the author only to find she’s recently passed away and he needs to find her daughter to gain the film rights. She has gone missing, and no-one knows where, until a break in at her mother’s flat leaves a clue that she might be living somewhere above ground. In an unlikely pairing with Rose Leonard, caretaker for and friend of the author, they go to find out what they can.<br /><br />It’s not a good time to be discovering the Roofworld, however. There is quite a sizeable community living above their heads and, as with any community, there is disagreement between them. 2 factions are battling for control. One, led by Zalian, an ex-alcoholic, ex-doctor, drug addict (strangely, these are the good guys!) are aiming to keep the Roofworld as it is, a Utopian escape from the madness that pervades the streets below. They are battling with a group led by one of his former disciples, Chymes, who has stolen a number of Zalian's men with promises of sex and drugs, but who ultimately wants control of the Roofworld all for himself.<br /><br />As Zalian’s power weakens so Chymes’ increases. His hunger for success is such, however, that he is bringing the Roofworld to the attention of the police down below, scattering bodies all over the streets of London, many killed in rather nasty ways. The interference in the battle for the Roofworld is not welcomed; neither is the presence of the outsiders who are merely after information.<br /><br />Whilst this is not one of Fowler’s more accomplished works, he knows how to keep the pages turning. The pace of the action is high, helped along by Fowler’s usual style of writing in short sharp chapters as the action sweeps from roof to roof across the whole of the City of London. Although characterised as a horror novelist, this is really a thriller, with only a couple of rather nasty methods of death and a little dark mysticism giving a nod towards traditional horror subjects.<br /><br />Although he knows how to write events superbly, Fowler’s main failings are apparent here. His characterisation is not good. Robert is almost an anti-hero, which makes it difficult for you to be on his side and the reasons for Rose joining up with him aren’t really explained. The Police get a bit of a rough ride as well, seemingly only being involved for the odd comic interlude, which detracts a little from the main story. For regular readers of Fowler’s work, though, a couple of the names will be familiar. The end is trademark Fowler as well – dragging on for a few pages longer than it needed, and being so weak that it almost undermines the suspense and action that has gone before.<br /><br />The true beauty of Christopher Fowler in general, and this is certainly true of “Roofworld”, is his seemingly encyclopaedic knowledge of London’s past and present. It is not only the story that will grab you, but the feeling that it is possible that a secret society could be living above our heads. After reading “Roofworld”, it suddenly becomes very difficult to avoid walking around a city without trying to spot the wires they would use to travel from place to place attached to the buildings. Particularly if you live and work in London and pass some of the places mentioned in the novel.<br /><br />I would hesitate to recommend this novel as a first dip into the works of Fowler, as his standard weaknesses may be a little off putting. Both “Disturbia” and “Soho Black” would be a better first read of his work. Once these have been enjoyed, however, I would recommend that “Roofworld” is tried, as it also contains everything that makes Fowler great.<br /><br />Fowler’s novels can be a little hard to find on the streets of London, although I have no idea of the availability on the rooftops. It's well worth looking for a novel that could give you neck strain to last a lifetime.<br /><br />This review may also appear under my name at any or all of <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.ciao.co.uk" target="_blank">www.ciao.co.uk</a>, <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.thebookbag.co.uk" target="_blank">www.thebookbag.co.uk</a>, <a href="http://www.goodreads.com" target="_blank">www.goodreads.com</a>, <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.amazon.co.uk" target="_blank">www.amazon.co.uk</a> and <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.dooyoo.co.uk" target="_blank">www.dooyoo.co.uk</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7688837635814935422">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/526632374?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 05, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1617945-lee" class="user" title="Lee">Lee</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/17333138-roofworld" title=" " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer526632374">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10841597990961184734">This is the first thing I've read from Mr.Fowler, and his first stand alone is an inventive adventure above the London skyline. With a sadistic psycho leader, with a cult following, and another more peaceful group that zip along cables between the rooftops, a battle is brewing. Rather unique concept, and pretty well written. 3.7 stars</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_361798396">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/361798396" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5809296-helen" class="left imgcol" title="Helen"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Helen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/361798396?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 05, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5809296-helen" class="user" title="Helen">Helen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/965402.Roofworld" title="Paperback 9780099623403" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5809296-helen?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5809296-helen?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer361798396">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13524136454902298622">I've been hoping to read this for some time and finally found a copy at Powell's in Portland. The story is set in London the week before Christmas and it is important to remember that it was written in the late 1980's because there are no cell phones and the computers are a little strange. A few of the characters Chris Fowler uses now in his Bryant and May series, like Sergeant Janice Longbright, the crabby coroner and poor clumsy Bimsly, appear but the most important element is the roofs of Lon</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13524136454902298622">I've been hoping to read this for some time and finally found a copy at Powell's in Portland. The story is set in London the week before Christmas and it is important to remember that it was written in the late 1980's because there are no cell phones and the computers are a little strange. A few of the characters Chris Fowler uses now in his Bryant and May series, like Sergeant Janice Longbright, the crabby coroner and poor clumsy Bimsly, appear but the most important element is the roofs of London where all the action takes place. We have a villain aiming at taking over the world from the roof down and another leader who just wants to live and let live. The rest of London has no idea that there is a whole culture living above them because "no one ever looks up". Given the weather it's not surprising since you'd be sure to get dirty water in your eye if you raised your eyes above the pavement. The story swoops along, very exciting in places, but the plot seems a little weak. I made a point of not slowing down to ask questions because I was enjoying it so much.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13524136454902298622">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <a href="/user/show/7038421-isabel-kittiwake" class="left imgcol" title="Isabel (kittiwake)"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1322770851p2/7038421.jpg" alt="Isabel (kittiwake)" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/242637893?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 03, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7038421-isabel-kittiwake" class="user" title="Isabel (kittiwake)">Isabel (kittiwake)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/965402.Roofworld" title="Paperback 9780099623403" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer242637893">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14605983754184709410"><em>The night was clear and cool and Rose was alone on the rooftops of London. It seemed peaceful to be above the city and yet still be a part of it. Carefully, she lined her camera up to the ledge along the building and looked through her viewfinder once more. But this time, instead of seeing the empty roofscape of the city, she saw the strangest thing. Running figures, some loping, some as if swinging on invisible ropes, bounding and leaping across the angled rooftops on the other side of the stre</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14605983754184709410"><em>The night was clear and cool and Rose was alone on the rooftops of London. It seemed peaceful to be above the city and yet still be a part of it. Carefully, she lined her camera up to the ledge along the building and looked through her viewfinder once more. But this time, instead of seeing the empty roofscape of the city, she saw the strangest thing. Running figures, some loping, some as if swinging on invisible ropes, bounding and leaping across the angled rooftops on the other side of the street. there were perhaps fifteen of them, young, old, men or women, it was difficult to tell. there was even a dog of some kind, darting silently between their feet.</em><br /><br />Since the 1920s the rooftops of London have been home to a well-organised society. Hundreds of people at a time have lived up there in secret, travelling along cables that they had strung between the buildings. But now, their world is crumbling around them as they fight for survival against the evil Chymes and his gang, while down below the police are starting to wonder what is going on, when several dead bodies are found that seem to have been killed up above. This book is set in the late 1980s. and even then the inhabitants of Roofworld were seeing more security cameras appearing on the rooftops. I'm not sure that the story would be feasible if it was set nowadays with so many CCTV cameras everywhere. People may not look upwards, but the cameras see everything.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14605983754184709410">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1294735183">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1294735183" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/955919-lisa-murray" class="left imgcol" title="Lisa Murray"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1300207874p2/955919.jpg" alt="Lisa Murray" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1294735183?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 31, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/955919-lisa-murray" class="user" title="Lisa Murray">Lisa Murray</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/955919-lisa-murray?shelf=mystery" class="actionLinkLite">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1294735183">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16308994760684894762">How odd to read Fowler's first novel after devouring all 10 of his Bryant &amp; May series. I could see the germination for the later series embedded here. For example, at one point a character laments the passing of a time when detectives were "idea men." That tension between detectives fitting productivity protocols and following their instincts is already evolving in this strange exploration of a fictional society that lives on the edges.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer21873149">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6108355569757642856">A great example of the hidden world trope. There's a secret society hiding more-or-less in plain sight and living among London's roof tops.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('AXpjUiCOeLoLmRqc48iPJeqCxUBaLxnZWWVax2tw7plkrlyB0lxsQIdBbiedqWmXo3UGWRPyXnMzbF9Vp+UY3A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Now, best-selling author and lifelong practitioner Stanley Bing enters the field with a comprehensive look at the many attractive jobs now available to those who are serious about their bullshit and prepared to dedicate their working life to it.\n\nWhat, Bing inquires, do a feng shui consultant, new media executive, wine steward, department store greeter, and Vice President of the United States have in common? What, too, are the actual duties performed by a McKinsey consultant? Other than sitting around making people nervous? Could that possibly be his core function? Likewise, what does an aromatherapist actually do, per se? Sniff things and rub them on people, for big fragrant bucks? Is that all?\n\nThe answer in all cases is &amp;quot;Yes.&amp;quot; They all have bullshit jobs.\n\nThese few, of course, are just the beginning. Across the length and breadth of this shrinking globe, skillful bullshit artists have secured pleasant, lucrative employment, and are enjoying themselves more than you are. In virtually every occupation, from Advertising to Yoga Franchising, lucky individuals who &amp;quot;work&amp;quot; in these coveted positions enjoy the best lives imaginable -- they are paid well, they rarely break a sweat, and their professions are highly respected, because nobody really knows what they do.\n\nAt once funny, useful, and tolerably philosophical, this groundbreaking work takes a close look at 100 bullshit jobs -- the money they bring with them, the actual tasks and activities involved (if any), and famous and successful examples of each position, who will provide the neophyte with inspiration. Most crucially, Bing goes on to offer what others so far have not--a clear, concise strategy to help job-seekers at every level reach for that brass ring, knowing full well that it may be attached to To anyone familiar with this writer -- whom the New York Times calls \"taut, chillingly ironic,\" the Washington Post Book W&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText159571055461728611\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;With each new novel, Dennis Cooper\'s reputation as the most daring and distinctive writer working in America today is cemented. To anyone familiar with this writer -- whom the New York Times calls &amp;quot;taut, chillingly ironic,&amp;quot; the Washington Post Book World terms &amp;quot;brilliant,&amp;quot; and the Village Voice deems capable of &amp;quot;religious intensity&amp;quot; -- it will come as no surprise that before he achieved success as a novelist, Dennis Cooper was best known as a poet. The Dream Police collects the best poems from five of his previous books and also includes a selection of new works. From his darkly erotic early verse to the more refined, post-punk poems that led critics to dub him &amp;quot;the spokesman for the Blank Generation,&amp;quot; to his later experimental pieces, Cooper\'s evolving study of the distances and dangers in romantic relationships has made him a singular voice in American poetry. The Dream Police is a vital addition to Dennis Cooper\'s riveting and disarming vision of life, love, obsession, and the depths of human need. &amp;quot;There can be no doubt about the power and originality of Cooper\'s writing.&amp;quot; -- The Washington Post Book World; &amp;quot;Cooper\'s vision is at first intense, nearly minimal, then suddenly it ascends into vision.&amp;quot; -- Kathy Acker; &amp;quot;In another country or another era, Dennis Cooper\'s books would be circulated in secret, explosive samizdat editions that friends and fans would pass around and savor like forbidden absinthe. He would risk his life for them, or maybe he\'d just be sent to a mental asylum, like the Marquis de Sade, to whom he has been compared. This is high risk literature. It takes enormous courage for a writer to explore, as Mr. Cooper does, the extreme boundaries of human behavior and amorality, right to the abyss where desire and lust topple to death.&amp;quot; --     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3912.Christopher_Fowler">About Christopher Fowler</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer3039068656553375347">Christopher Fowler is an English novelist living in London, his books contain elements of black comedy, anxiety and social satire. As well as novels, he writes short stories, scripts, press articles and reviews.<br /><br />He lives in King's Cross, on the Battlebridge Basin, and chooses London as the backdrop of many of his stories because any one of the events in its two thousand year history can provide ins</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3039068656553375347">Christopher Fowler is an English novelist living in London, his books contain elements of black comedy, anxiety and social satire. As well as novels, he writes short stories, scripts, press articles and reviews.<br /><br />He lives in King's Cross, on the Battlebridge Basin, and chooses London as the backdrop of many of his stories because any one of the events in its two thousand year history can provide inspiration<br /><br />In 1998 he was the recipient of the BFS Best Short Story Of The Year, for 'Wageslaves'. Then, in 2004, 'The Water Room' was nominated for the CWA People's Choice Award, 'Full Dark House' won the BFS August Derleth Novel of The Year Award 2004 and 'American Waitress' won the BFS Best Short Story Of The Year 2004. The novella 'Breathe' won BFS Best Novella 2005.<br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.peculiarcrimesunit.com/" target="_blank">http://www.peculiarcrimesunit.com/</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3039068656553375347">...more</a>

    </div>
